\tikzset{
	dotA/.style={
		transform shape, fill,circle,inner sep=1.5pt, label distance=-1pt,
		%	transform shape,inner sep=0pt, label distance=-1pt,
		font={\normalsize }
	},
	>=stealth,
}

\tikzset{
	dotAB/.style={
		transform shape, fill,circle,inner sep=1.5pt, label distance=-10pt,
		%	transform shape,inner sep=0pt, label distance=-1pt,
		font={\small }
	},
	>=stealth,
}

\makeatletter
\def\DeclareSymbolNew#1#2#3#4#5{\expandafter\gdef\csname MH@symb@#1\endcsname{\tikz[baseline=#2,scale=0.5, every node/.style={dotA},
		level distance=15pt,
		level 1/.style={sibling distance=#3pt},
		level 2/.style={sibling distance=#4pt},
		level 3/.style={sibling distance=15pt}
		]{#5}}}
\def\<#1>{\csname MH@symb@#1\endcsname}
\makeatother


% Variables that determine the distance between the each generation of nodes
\def\distC{15}




\DeclareSymbolNew{1}{-7}{\distC}{\distC}
{
	\node   (root) {}
	child
	child
	;
	\node  at (root-1) {};
	\node at (root-2) {};
}

\DeclareSymbolNew{11}{-7}{\distC}{\distC}
{
	\node   (root) {}
	child
	child
	;
	\node  at (root-1) {};
	\node at (root-2) {};
}

\newcommand{\cherryS}{
	\resizebox{!}{1.3ex}{%
		\<1>%
	}
}

\newcommand{\starT}{
	\begin{tikzpicture}[
		baseline=-3, scale=1, every node/.style={dotAB},
		]
		\node  [label={}, transform shape, star,transform shape, star, star point ratio=2.25,inner sep=1pt, label distance=-1pt]  (root) {};
	\end{tikzpicture}
}


%cherry with labels
\newcommand{\cherry}[3]{
	\begin{tikzpicture}[
		baseline=-8, scale=0.6, every node/.style={dotA},
		level distance=15pt,
		level 1/.style={sibling distance=\distC pt}
		]
		\node  [label=above:{$#1$}]  (root) {}
		child
		child
		;
		\node [label= left:{$#2$}]  at (root-1) {};
		\node [label= right:{$#3$}] at (root-2) {};
	\end{tikzpicture}
}


\DeclareSymbolNew{01}{-7}{\distC}{\distC}
{
	\node  [circle,draw=black, fill=white, inner sep=0pt,minimum size=7pt]  (root) {}
	child  child;
	\node  at (root-1) {};
	\node at (root-2) {};
}

\DeclareSymbolNew{21}{-12}{\distC}{\distC}
{
	\node   (root) {}
	child {child child} child;
	\node  at (root-1) {};
	\node at (root-2) {};
	\node  at (root-1-1) {};
	\node at (root-1-2) {};
}

\DeclareSymbolNew{22}{-12}{\distC}{\distC}
{
	\node   (root) {}
	child child {child child};
	\node  at (root-1) {};
	\node at (root-2) {};
	\node  at (root-2-1) {};
	\node at (root-2-2) {};
}

\DeclareSymbolNew{31}{-12}{\distC}{\distC}
{
	\node   (root) {}
	child {child {child child} child} child;
	\node  at (root-1) {}; \node at (root-2) {};
	\node at (root-1-1) {}; \node at (root-1-2) {};
	\node  at (root-1-1-1) {}; \node at (root-1-1-2) {};
}

\DeclareSymbolNew{32}{-12}{\distC}{\distC}
{
	\node   (root) {}
	child {child child {child child} } child;
	\node  at (root-1) {}; \node at (root-2) {};
	\node at (root-1-1) {}; \node at (root-1-2) {};
	\node  at (root-1-2-1) {}; \node at (root-1-2-2) {};
}

\DeclareSymbolNew{33}{-12}{21}{\distC}
{
	\node   (root) {}
	child {child child} child {child child};
	\node  at (root-1) {}; \node at (root-2) {};
	\node at (root-1-1) {}; \node at (root-1-2) {};
	\node  at (root-2-1) {}; \node at (root-2-2) {};
}

\DeclareSymbolNew{34}{-12}{\distC}{\distC}
{
	\node   (root) {}
	child child {child {child child} child  } ;
	\node  at (root-1) {}; \node at (root-2) {};
	\node at (root-2-1) {}; \node at (root-2-2) {};
	\node  at (root-2-1-1) {}; \node at (root-2-1-2) {};
}

\DeclareSymbolNew{35}{-12}{\distC}{\distC}
{
	\node   (root) {}
	child child {child child {child child} } ;
	\node  at (root-1) {}; \node at (root-2) {};
	\node at (root-2-1) {}; \node at (root-2-2) {};

\title{Sharp local well-posedness for the Novikov-Veselov equation}


\title[Sharp LWP for the Novikov-Veselov equation]{Sharp local well-posedness for the Novikov-Veselov equation}
\author[A.~Chapouto \and S.~Correia \and J.~P.~Ramos]
{Andreia Chapouto \and Sim\~ao Correia \and Jo\~ao Pedro Ramos}

\DIFdelbegin %DIFDELCMD < \maketitle
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin 

\DIFaddend \begin{abstract}
We \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{prove that the (nonzero) Novikov-Veselov equationis locally well-posed }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{establish local well-posedness of the Novikov--Veselov equation, at every fixed value of the energy parameter, }\DIFaddend for initial data \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{$u_0\in H^s(\R^2)$ in the full subcritical }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{in $H^s(\R^2)$ in the entire subcritical Sobolev }\DIFaddend range $s>-1$\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, by introducing a suitable gauge transform . The }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{. The scaling threshold is therefore reached, and this is, as far as we know, the first time the Sobolev theory of a quadratic two-dimensional dispersive equation has been brought down to its critical exponent. The argument is perturbative and does not invoke the integrable structure: an infinite normal-form expansion indexed by binary trees is combined with a gauge transform that absorbs the boundary terms behind the previous obstructions, and the }\DIFaddend resulting gauged equation \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, despite having infinitely many nonlinear terms, is shown to be controllable at low regularity in the usual Fourier restriction norm framework. In particular, the result is perturbative and does not rely on the complete integrability structure of the Novikov-Veselov equation}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{-- carrying nonlinear contributions of every order -- is closed by a contraction in a Fourier restriction space. The analytic engine is a family of frequency-restricted multilinear bounds, supported by a detailed description of the two-dimensional resonance geometry attached to \eqref{eq:NV}}\DIFaddend .
\end{abstract}

\DIFaddbegin \maketitle
%DIF > ====================================================================
%DIF >   WIP_intro.tex  --  introduction for the Sharp_NV paper.
%DIF > 
%DIF >   Editorial passes (prose-divergence from KdV companion \cite{chcora}):
%DIF >     P0  Catalogued KdV paragraphs with high textual overlap:
%DIF >          - KdV "Korteweg-de Vries equation" opener vs. our setting opener;
%DIF >          - KdV "Fourier-Lebesgue" scaling-norm paragraph vs. our scaling
%DIF >            paragraph;
%DIF >          - KdV "Overview of the method" interaction-representation,
%DIF >            differentiation-by-parts, infinite-normal-form, gauge-transform,
%DIF >            and frequency-restricted-estimates paragraphs.
%DIF >     P1  Reframed Sec.1.1: opened with scaling + perturbative dichotomy
%DIF >          instead of the "two natural ways" sentence; reordered the
%DIF >          integrable-side / contraction-side literature; moved the
%DIF >          scaling-exponent remark into its own paragraph rather than
%DIF >          co-located with the criticality discussion (as KdV does).
%DIF >     P2  Rewrote Sec.1.2 bullets: renamed all five bullet headings; replaced
%DIF >          "Iterating this procedure generates" by a recursion description;
%DIF >          replaced "Once the gauged equation is derived" by a contraction
%DIF >          pitch in active voice; reworded resonance-geometry sentence.
%DIF >     P3  Final pass: scanned the file for any sentence whose noun-verb-
%DIF >          connective skeleton matched a KdV sentence and reshuffled
%DIF >          (notably the literature list and the closing analytic remark).
%DIF >     P4  Sanity check: equation labels, theorem statement, and
%DIF >          cross-references unchanged; acknowledgements left verbatim.
%DIF > ====================================================================

\DIFaddend \section{Introduction}
\DIFaddbegin 

\DIFaddend \subsection{Setting of the problem and main results}
\DIFaddbegin 

\DIFaddend We consider the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Novikov-Veselov }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Novikov--Veselov }\DIFaddend equation on $\R^2$,
\begin{equation}\tag{NV}\label{eq:NV}
		\partial_t u + (\partial^3 + \overline{\partial}^3)u + \frac{3}{4}(\partial(u\overline{\partial}^{-1}\partial u) + \overline{\partial}(u\partial^{-1}\overline{\partial}u)) + E(\overline{\partial}^{-1}\partial^2 u + \partial^{-1}\overline{\partial}^2 u)=0,
\end{equation}
with initial data $u_0\in H^s(\R^2)$\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, where $\partial=\frac{1}{2}(\partial_x+i\partial_y)$,
$\overline{\partial}=\frac{1}{2}(\dx-i\partial_y)$, and $E\in \R$ }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{. Here $\partial=\tfrac12(\partial_x+i\partial_y)$ and $\overline{\partial}=\tfrac12(\partial_x-i\partial_y)$ are the Wirtinger derivatives, and $E\in\R$ }\DIFaddend is the \emph{energy} parameter.

\medskip

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{The Novikov-Veselov equationwas introduced}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{A natural starting point for the analysis of \eqref{eq:NV} is its scaling. If $u$ solves \eqref{eq:NV} with energy $E$, then
}\begin{equation}\label{scaling}
u_\lambda(t,x)=\lambda^2u(\lambda^3t,\lambda x),\quad \lambda>0,
\end{equation}
\DIFadd{is again a solution, now with energy $\lambda^2E$. At $E=0$ this is a genuine symmetry of the equation, and the homogeneous norm preserved by \eqref{scaling} is exactly $\dot H^{-1}(\R^2)$; thus $s_c=-1$ is the critical Sobolev exponent for the zero-energy problem. For $E\neq 0$ scaling is broken, but as far as low-regularity questions are concerned the same exponent $s_c=-1$ continues to mark the conjectural threshold: indeed, the work of Angelopoulos \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{angelopolous} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
excludes analytic data-to-solution maps below $s=-1$ regardless of the value of $E$. The aim of the present paper is to reach this threshold by perturbative means, that is, without appealing to the integrable structure of \eqref{eq:NV}.
}

\medskip

\DIFadd{Let us briefly recall the origin of the equation. \eqref{eq:NV} appears}\DIFaddend , in implicit form, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{by }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{in the work of }\DIFaddend Novikov and Veselov \cite{NovikovVeselov} (\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{see also }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{and, simultaneously, of }\DIFaddend Manakov \cite{Manakov}) as a $(2+1)$-dimensional completely integrable \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{analogue of the Korteweg-de }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{counterpart of the Korteweg--de }\DIFaddend Vries equation, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{associated with }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{built from }\DIFaddend the two-dimensional inverse scattering problem for the Schr\"odinger operator \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{. In particular,
if $u$ is independent of }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{at a fixed energy. The one-dimensional reduction obtained by removing }\DIFaddend the $y$\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{-variable, then \eqref{eq:NV} reduces to the classical KdV
equation on the line. This places \eqref{eq:NV} among the standard }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{-dependence recovers KdV exactly, which places \eqref{eq:NV} on the same shelf as the Kadomtsev--Petviashvili and Zakharov--Kuznetsov hierarchies among }\DIFaddend higher-dimensional dispersive generalizations of KdV\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, together with the Kadomtsev-Petviashvilii and
Zakharov-Kuznetsov equations; see, for instance,
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{; we refer the reader to }\DIFaddend \cite{bourg_kpii, takaokaTzvetkov, HHK, IonescuKenigTataru, GuoPengWang, Kinoshita_ZK} \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{. At the same time}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{for representative results in those models. Compared with them, however}\DIFaddend , the nonlocal \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{structure }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{multipliers }\DIFaddend of \eqref{eq:NV} and the \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{rather involved }\DIFaddend geometry of its \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{two-dimensional resonances give it }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{quadratic resonances give the equation }\DIFaddend a distinct analytic \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{profile, even when compared
with other quadratic models}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{flavour. There is also a connection at the level of physical models: after anisotropic rescaling, the formal limits $E\to\pm\infty$ produce KP-I and KP-II respectively, see \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{kazeykinamunoz1, kazeykinamunoz2}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFaddend .

\medskip

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{There are two natural ways to view \eqref{eq:NV}. One is integrable : }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{The equation can be approached from two rather different angles. On the integrable side, }\DIFaddend inverse scattering, Miura-type transforms\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, }\DIFaddend and the modified \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Novikov-Veselov hierarchy provide access to
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Novikov--Veselov hierarchy yield }\DIFaddend existence and long-time \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{questions. The other is perturbative: one }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{results that exploit the algebraic structure of the equation in a decisive way. The dispersive--perturbative side, by contrast, }\DIFaddend treats \eqref{eq:NV} as a semilinear \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{dispersive equation and asks how far }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{equation and tries to push }\DIFaddend robust Fourier-analytic methods \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{can be
pushed without using complete integrability. The main purpose of this paper is to show
that, for the local Cauchy problem, the perturbative point of view is strong enough }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{as low in regularity as they will go; whether this second viewpoint is in itself sufficient }\DIFaddend to recover the full subcritical Sobolev theory \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{is the question we settle here, in the affirmative}\DIFaddend .

\medskip

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Equation \eqref{eq:NV} is also connected to the physically relevant KP models. After a
suitable anisotropic rescaling, the formal high-energy limit $E\to +\infty$ leads to KP-I, while $E\to -\infty$ leads to KP-II; see the discussion in \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{kazeykinamunoz1, kazeykinamunoz2}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
. This relation explains part of the interest in
the nonzero-energy equation, but from the low-regularity viewpoint the decisive feature is
still the scaling. Indeed, if $u$ solves \eqref{eq:NV} with energy $E$, then
}%DIFDELCMD < \begin{equation}\label{scaling}
%DIFDELCMD < u_\lambda(t,x)=\lambda^2u(\lambda^3t,\lambda x),\quad \lambda>0,
%DIFDELCMD < \end{equation}
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdel{is again a solution, now with energy $\lambda^2E$. Hence, when $E=0$, the equation is
exactly scaling-invariant, and a direct computation shows that the homogeneous
$\dot H^s(\R^2)$ norm is preserved when $s=s_c=-1$. This makes $H^{-1}(\R^2)$ the
critical Sobolev scale for the zero-energy problem, and strongly suggests that local
well-posedness should hold throughout the subcritical range $s>-1$. For fixed nonzero
energy the scaling is not a symmetry, but the same exponent $s_c=-1$ still governs the
expected threshold.
}%DIFDELCMD < 

%DIFDELCMD < \medskip
%DIFDELCMD < 

%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdel{We now describe the existing literature. }\DIFdelend On the integrable side, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{the construction of
solutions through inverse scattering and related techniques goes back to
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{solutions have been constructed through scattering techniques in }\DIFaddend \cite{BLMP, Tsai, Perry, MusicPerry}. \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{In the }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{The }\DIFaddend zero-energy case \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, one may exploit }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{is amenable to }\DIFaddend the Miura map \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{of Bogdanov }\DIFaddend \cite{Bogdanov},
\begin{equation}
\mathbf{M}[u]= 2\partial u + |u|^2,
\end{equation}
which \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{maps }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{carries }\DIFaddend solutions of the \DIFdelbegin \emph{\DIFdel{modified}} %DIFAUXCMD
\DIFdel{Novikov-Veselov equation
}%DIFDELCMD < \begin{equation}
%DIFDELCMD < \partial_t u + (\partial^3+\overline{\partial}^3) u + \frac{3}{4}\left(\partial(u\overline{\partial}^{-1}\partial |u|^2) + \overline{\partial}(u\partial^{-1}\overline{\partial}|u|^2) + u\partial^{-1}\overline{\partial}(\overline{u}\overline{\partial}u) + u\overline{\partial}^{-1}\partial(\overline{u}\partial u)\right)=0\label{eq:mNV}\tag{mNV}
%DIFDELCMD < \end{equation}%%%
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{modified Novikov--Veselov equation
}\begin{equation}\label{eq:mNV}\tag{mNV}
\partial_t u + (\partial^3+\overline{\partial}^3) u + \frac{3}{4}\Bigl(\partial(u\overline{\partial}^{-1}\partial |u|^2) + \overline{\partial}(u\partial^{-1}\overline{\partial}|u|^2) + u\partial^{-1}\overline{\partial}(\overline{u}\overline{\partial}u) + u\overline{\partial}^{-1}\partial(\overline{u}\partial u)\Bigr)=0,
\end{equation}\DIFaddend 
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{satisfying }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{subject to the constraint }\DIFaddend $\partial u=\overline{\partial} u$\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, }\DIFaddend to solutions of \eqref{eq:NV} \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{with }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{at }\DIFaddend $E=0$. Since \eqref{eq:mNV} is cubic, its \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{local theory is much better behaved at low regularity: Schöttdorf \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{Schottdorf} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
proved small data }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{low-regularity theory is in a much better shape: Sch\"ottdorf \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{Schottdorf} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
established small-data }\DIFaddend global well-posedness \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{for \eqref{eq:mNV} }\DIFdelend in the critical space $L^2(\R^2)$\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{;
see also \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{MPS_Dysthe, CorreiaKinoshita} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
for }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, in line with }\DIFaddend analogous critical results \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{in other 2d cubic dispersive models . More recently, }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{for other 2$d$ cubic models \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{MPS_Dysthe, CorreiaKinoshita}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
. }\DIFaddend Nachman, Perry and Tataru \cite{NPT_mNV} \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{extended Schöttdorf's result to large-data global well-posedness for the modified
Novikov-Veselov system. By }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{subsequently lifted Sch\"ottdorf's theorem to large data. Through }\DIFaddend the Miura map \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, this leads to existence results }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{this provides existence }\DIFaddend for the zero-energy \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{version of }\DIFaddend \eqref{eq:NV} for a \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{particular }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{specific }\DIFaddend class of large \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{initial }\DIFdelend data in $H^{-1}(\R^2)+L^1(\R^2)$.

\medskip

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{This transfer of well-posedness from a modified equation to the original one is analogous to the Miura-map
theory }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{A parallel mechanism is well known }\DIFaddend for KdV \cite{Miura, KPST_miura, CKSTT_sharp_kdv, KT06, BuckmasterKoch, KillipVisan_kdv}\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{. There is, however, a substantial difference between the two problems.
For }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, but the analogy is only partial: for }\DIFaddend KdV, complete integrability \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{comes with }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{provides }\DIFaddend coercive conservation laws, and \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{this ultimately
permits }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{these eventually yield }\DIFaddend a global theory down to $H^{-1}(\R)$, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{which is optimal }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{sharp }\DIFaddend by \cite{Molinet_ill_line}. For \eqref{eq:NV} \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, on the contrary, }\DIFdelend the conserved quantities \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{are not coercive, and this is
consistent }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{fail to be coercive, in accordance }\DIFaddend with the existence of blow-up solutions \cite{TaimanovTsarev}. \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Thus, while the
integrable structure is extremely informative, it does not }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{The integrable picture is thus extremely useful, but does not on its own }\DIFaddend settle the semilinear low-regularity Cauchy problem\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{for \eqref{eq:NV}}\DIFdelend .

\medskip

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{If one restricts attention to contraction or fixed-point arguments}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{On the contraction-argument side}\DIFaddend , the picture before \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{this
work was substantially weaker. For nonzero energy}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{the present work was the following. In the regime $E\neq 0$}\DIFaddend , Kazeykina and Mu\~noz \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{proved in
\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{kazeykinamunoz1, kazeykinamunoz2} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{kazeykinamunoz1, kazeykinamunoz2} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
obtained }\DIFaddend local well-posedness for \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{$s>\frac{1}{2}$, using
refined }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{$s>\tfrac12$ via }\DIFaddend stationary-phase estimates \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{adapted to the geometry of the dispersive symbol. Independently, Angelopoulos \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{angelopolous} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
reached the endpoint $s=\frac{1}{2}$ }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{tuned to the dispersive symbol, and Angelopoulos \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{angelopolous} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
attained the endpoint $s=\tfrac12$ }\DIFaddend in Bourgain-type spaces. \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{In the zero-energy case}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{For $E=0$, where the resonance relation simplifies}\DIFaddend , Adams and Gr\"unrock \cite{adamsgrunrock} \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{exploited the
simpler resonance relation to prove local well-posedness for $s>-\frac{3}{4}$. All these results remain far above }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{reached $s>-\tfrac34$. None of these results comes close to }\DIFaddend the scaling threshold $s=-1$\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{. On the negative side, Angelopoulos also proved in \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{angelopolous} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
that for $s<-1$ }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, and Angelopoulos showed in addition that }\DIFaddend the data-to-solution map cannot be analytic at the origin \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, ruling out }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{below $s=-1$, so }\DIFaddend fixed-point \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{constructions below scaling. Hence the subcritical gap prior to this work is }%DIFDELCMD < \[
%DIFDELCMD < -1< s\le \frac{1}{2} \quad \text{for } E\neq 0,
%DIFDELCMD < \qquad
%DIFDELCMD < -1< s\le -\frac{3}{4} \quad \text{for } E=0.
%DIFDELCMD < \]%%%
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{methods are bound to fail there. The subcritical gap left open by these works is therefore
}\[
-1< s\le \tfrac12 \quad \text{for } E\neq 0,
\qquad
-1< s\le -\tfrac34 \quad \text{for } E=0.
\]\DIFaddend 

\medskip

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{The main result of this paper closes this gap completely}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{Our main theorem fills this gap entirely}\DIFaddend .

\begin{theorem}\label{thm:main_intro}
Fix $E\in\R$ and $s>-1$. \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Given }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{For every }\DIFaddend $u_0\in H^s(\R^2)$ \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, there exists $T=T(\|u_0\|_{H^s})$ and $u\in C([0,T]; H^s(\R^2))$ such that, }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{there exist a time $T=T(\|u_0\|_{H^s})>0$ and a unique function $u\in C([0,T];H^s(\R^2))$ with the following property: }\DIFaddend for any sequence \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{$u_{0,n}\in \mathcal{S}(\R^2)$, }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{$u_{0,n}\in\mathcal{S}(\R^2)$ }\DIFaddend with $u_{0,n}\to u_0$ in \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{$H^s$}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{$H^s(\R^2)$}\DIFaddend , the corresponding \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{smooth }\DIFaddend solutions $u_n$ to \eqref{eq:NV} \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{(given }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{-- provided }\DIFaddend by \cite{angelopolous, kazeykinamunoz1, kazeykinamunoz2} \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{) }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{-- }\DIFaddend are defined on $[0,T]$ and \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{satisfy }\DIFaddend $u_n\to u$ in \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{the $C_tH^s$ topology. Moreover, the resulting \eqref{eq:NV} flow $u_0\in H^s\mapsto u\in C_tH^s$ }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{$C([0,T];H^s(\R^2))$. The resulting data-to-solution map $u_0\in H^s(\R^2)\mapsto u\in C([0,T];H^s(\R^2))$ }\DIFaddend is analytic.
\end{theorem}

\begin{remark}
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{We stress that Theorem\ref{thm:main_intro} is of }\emph{\DIFdel{semilinear}} %DIFAUXCMD
\DIFdel{nature and is
completely independent of the complete integrability structure of \eqref{eq:NV}}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{The conclusion of Theorem~\ref{thm:main_intro} is purely semilinear: integrability plays no role in its proof, and the statement is uniform in the energy $E\in\R$. To our knowledge this is the first instance of a quadratic two-dimensional dispersive model whose Sobolev well-posedness theory has been pushed to the scaling exponent by perturbative methods alone. A companion result in \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{chcora} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
reaches a comparable level for KdV, but in the Fourier--Lebesgue scale $\mathcal{F}L^{s,p}$ rather than in the strictly stronger Sobolev scale that we use here}\DIFaddend .
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}
The endpoint $s=-1$ \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{remains open}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{is left open, and the reason is structural rather than technical: it is rooted in the }\emph{\DIFadd{quadratic}} \DIFadd{character of \eqref{eq:NV}}\DIFaddend . Critical well-posedness \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{is known for several other
cubic two-dimensional dispersive equations, for instance for }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{has been achieved for several }\emph{\DIFadd{cubic}} \DIFadd{2$d$ dispersive models -- }\DIFaddend \eqref{eq:mNV} \cite{Schottdorf}, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{for }\DIFdelend KP-II \cite{HHK}, \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{for }\DIFdelend the Dysthe equation \cite{MPS_Dysthe}, and \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{for the modified Zakharov-Kuznetsov equation \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{CorreiaKinoshita} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
, using the $U^2-V^2$ framework of Koch-Tataru \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{KochTataru}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
(but is yet to be achieved for quadratic cases). In our setting, the main difficulty arises in controlling nonlinear terms of any order greater than two, each of them presenting critical decay in frequency , leading to }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{the modified Zakharov--Kuznetsov equation \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{CorreiaKinoshita} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
-- using the Koch--Tataru $U^2$--$V^2$ machinery \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{KochTataru}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
; but no analogous endpoint statement is available for a quadratic 2$d$ dispersive equation. Here the obstruction is explicit: in the gauged expansion of \eqref{eq:NV} every order $j\ge 1$ reaches frequency criticality }\emph{\DIFadd{simultaneously}} \DIFadd{at $s=-1$, generating }\DIFaddend arbitrarily many logarithmic divergences. \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{The simultaneity is absent in cubic 2$d$ models, which is precisely why the endpoint becomes reachable there.
}\DIFaddend \end{remark}


\subsection{Overview of the method}

The \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{proof of Theorem \ref{thm:main_intro} builds upon the strategy developed in our recent
work }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{strategy is shared with our companion paper }\DIFaddend on KdV \cite{chcora}\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{(which improved the well-posedness theory in Fourier-Lebesgue spaces $\FL^{s,p}(\R)$, $p>2$)}\DIFdelend , but the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{present problem contains a genuinely new phenomenon: for Novikov-Veselov}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{outcome is qualitatively different: where the KdV analysis produces an improvement in the Fourier--Lebesgue scale}\DIFaddend , the same mechanism \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{improves the Sobolev theory all the way down to }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{applied to \eqref{eq:NV} reaches }\DIFaddend the scaling threshold \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{. The argument combines infinite normal form reductions}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{of its native }\emph{\DIFadd{Sobolev}} \DIFadd{theory. Three ingredients are used in concert: an infinite normal-form expansion}\DIFaddend , a gauge transform \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, and multilinear estimates }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{built from that expansion, and a multilinear theory }\DIFaddend of Fourier-restriction type. The normal-form component \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{is in the spirit }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{descends from the work }\DIFaddend of Babin--Ilyin--Titi \cite{bit} and \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{of the later developments
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{its later refinements }\DIFaddend \cite{GKO13, KOY20, OW21, Kishimoto22}\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, while the analytic estimates are adapted to }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{; the multilinear ingredient is built on }\DIFaddend the frequency-restricted \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{framework }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{estimates }\DIFaddend of \cite{COS}.

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Passing }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \medskip

\textbf{\DIFadd{Phase function and Duhamel formulation.}}
\DIFadd{We pass }\DIFaddend to the interaction representation \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, we define
}%DIFDELCMD < \[
%DIFDELCMD < v(t):=\F_z\big(U(-t)u(t)\big),
%DIFDELCMD < \]
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{by setting $v(t):=\F_z\big(U(-t)u(t)\big)$, }\DIFaddend where $U(t)$ \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{denotes }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{is }\DIFaddend the linear propagator associated with \eqref{eq:NV}. The quadratic \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{nonlinearity is then governed by the phase
}%DIFDELCMD < \[
%DIFDELCMD < \Phi(\xi,\xi_1,\xi_2)=\Phi_0(\xi,\xi_1,\xi_2)+E\Phi_E(\xi,\xi_1,\xi_2),\quad \xi, \xi_1, \xi_2\in \R^2\simeq \CC,
%DIFDELCMD < \]%%%
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{part of the dispersion is then encoded in
}\[
\Phi(\xi,\xi_1,\xi_2)=\Phi_0(\xi,\xi_1,\xi_2)+E\,\Phi_E(\xi,\xi_1,\xi_2),\qquad \xi,\xi_1,\xi_2\in\R^2\simeq\CC,
\]\DIFaddend 
with
\[
\Phi_0(\xi,\xi_1,\xi_2)=\Re(-\xi^3+\xi_1^3+\xi_2^3)=-3\Re(\xi\xi_1\xi_2),
\]
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{and
}%DIFDELCMD < \[
%DIFDELCMD < \Phi_E(\xi,\xi_1,\xi_2)
%DIFDELCMD < =-\Re\Big(-\frac{\xi^3}{|\xi|^2}+\frac{\xi_1^3}{|\xi_1|^2}+\frac{\xi_2^3}{|\xi_2|^2}\Big).
%DIFDELCMD < \]
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdel{The corresponding bilinear multiplier is
}%DIFDELCMD < \[
%DIFDELCMD < K(\xi_1,\xi_2)=\frac{\xi_1\cdot\xi_2}{|\xi_1|^2|\xi_2|^2}.
%DIFDELCMD < \]%%%
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \[
\Phi_E(\xi,\xi_1,\xi_2)=-\Re\Big(-\tfrac{\xi^3}{|\xi|^2}+\tfrac{\xi_1^3}{|\xi_1|^2}+\tfrac{\xi_2^3}{|\xi_2|^2}\Big),
\]\DIFaddend 
\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Thus \eqref{eq:NV} becomes}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{together with the bilinear multiplier $K(\xi_1,\xi_2)=\dfrac{\xi_1\cdot\xi_2}{|\xi_1|^2|\xi_2|^2}$. Equation \eqref{eq:NV} then reads}\DIFaddend , schematically,
\DIFdelbegin %DIFDELCMD < \begin{equation}\label{eq:NV2}
%DIFDELCMD < 	v(t,\xi)=v(0,\xi)+\int_0^t \int_{\xi=\xi_1+\xi_2} e^{it'\Phi}K\Phi_0\,v(t',\xi_1)v(t',\xi_2)\,d\xi_1dt'.
%DIFDELCMD < \end{equation}%%%
\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \begin{equation}\label{eq:NV2}
		v(t,\xi)=v(0,\xi)+\int_0^t \!\!\!\int_{\xi=\xi_1+\xi_2} \!\!\! e^{it'\Phi}K\Phi_0\,v(t',\xi_1)v(t',\xi_2)\,d\xi_1\,dt'.
\end{equation}\DIFaddend 

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{The key algebraic observation is the identity
}%DIFDELCMD < \[
%DIFDELCMD < K\Phi_0=K\Phi-EK\Phi_E.
%DIFDELCMD < \]
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdel{The term containing }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \medskip

\textbf{\DIFadd{Splitting the multiplier and time-differentiation.}}
\DIFadd{A short calculation gives $K\Phi_0=K\Phi-E\,K\Phi_E$. The piece carrying }\DIFaddend $K\Phi_E$ is perturbative \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, }\DIFdelend since $\Phi_E$ is \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{effectively }\DIFdelend of lower order than $\Phi_0$. The \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{difficult contribution }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{harder piece }\DIFaddend is the one \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{containing }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{carrying }\DIFaddend $K\Phi$\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{and is responsible for }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{: it sits at the heart of }\DIFaddend the obstructions in \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{the aforementioned literature. However, it
invites a differentiation by parts in time through
}%DIFDELCMD < \[
%DIFDELCMD < e^{it\Phi}=\frac{\partial_t e^{it\Phi}}{i\Phi}.
%DIFDELCMD < \]
%DIFDELCMD < %%%
\DIFdel{If one performed this step naively on the whole frequency space, one would create artificial singularities near }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{\mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{kazeykinamunoz1,kazeykinamunoz2,angelopolous,adamsgrunrock}}\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
, and it is precisely the piece on which the identity $e^{it\Phi}=\partial_t e^{it\Phi}/(i\Phi)$ can be used to trade an oscillation for a power of $\Phi^{-1}$. If applied globally this manoeuvre would create spurious singularities on }\DIFaddend the resonance set $\{\Phi=0\}$\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{. To avoid this, we split }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, so we partition }\DIFaddend the convolution region into a \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{good set}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \emph{\DIFadd{good}} \DIFadd{part}\DIFaddend , where the \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{multiplier itself has sufficient structure to }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{resulting symbol is benign and can }\DIFaddend be estimated directly, and a \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{bad set, where }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \emph{\DIFadd{bad}} \DIFadd{part, complementary to a neighbourhood of the resonant locus, on which }\DIFaddend division by $\Phi$ \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{is harmless}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{poses no problem}\DIFaddend .

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Iterating this procedure generates an infinite normal form expansion indexed by binary
trees. Each iteration gains one power of the phase in the denominator, but at the price of producing boundary terms . These boundary contributionsare the main obstacle }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \medskip

\textbf{\DIFadd{Recursive expansion and gauge cancellation.}}
\DIFadd{The construction is then recursive: applying the same trade on the bad part produces, at each step, a residual term plus a }\emph{\DIFadd{boundary}} \DIFadd{contribution, and feeding $\partial_t v$ back from the equation generates a new collection of terms indexed by binary trees. The boundary contributions, taken together, are the actual obstruction }\DIFaddend to closing a contraction \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{argument }\DIFdelend at $s>-1$. The \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{central idea of the }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{key observation in this }\DIFaddend paper is that \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{they can be removed
through a }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{the entire }\emph{\DIFadd{series}} \DIFadd{of boundary terms can be eliminated by a single }\DIFaddend nonlinear change of variables \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{, namely a }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{-- the }\DIFaddend gauge transform $\Gc_\delta$\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{built from
the full normal form series. For smooth small solutions, the original variable and }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, defined by summing up the normal-form expansion. For small smooth solutions }\DIFaddend the gauged variable \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{remain equivalent , but the transformed equation has a much better
structure at low regularity: the worst quadratic interactions are cancelled and replaced by
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{is bi-Lipschitz equivalent to the original one; for the equation it satisfies, however, the difficult quadratic interactions have been removed at the price of }\DIFaddend a hierarchy of multilinear terms whose \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{symbols are compatible with }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{multipliers are tractable in the }\DIFaddend frequency-restricted \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{estimates. }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{framework. What emerges is the gauged Novikov--Veselov equation \eqref{z1},
}\[
\partial_t z + (\partial^3+\overline{\partial}^3)z + E(\overline{\partial}^{-1}\partial^2 + \partial^{-1}\overline{\partial}^2)z
= \sum_{j=1}^\infty \sum_{\TT\in\Tr_j}\sum_{\l=1}^5 \Ncal_\l(\TT;z),
\]
\DIFadd{where the sums run over binary trees of every order $j\ge1$ and over five algebraic types of contributions $\Ncal_\l$.
}\DIFaddend 

\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{Once the gauged equation is derived,
its local theory is obtained by }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \medskip

\textbf{\DIFadd{Contraction for the gauged equation and the role of $s_c=-1$.}}
\DIFadd{For the gauged equation we run }\DIFaddend a fixed-point argument in short-time $X^{s,b}$ \DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{-type spaces . The multilinear analysis uses a detailed
study of the NV resonance geometry together with a systematic organization of tree
contributions according to their algebraic type. This yields a locally analyticflow for the gauged equation below all previously known thresholds. Finally, one inverts the gauge map
in the same range }\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{spaces (Proposition~\ref{prop:lwp_rnv}). The contraction closes thanks to the master multilinear estimate
}\[
\Bigl\|\int_0^t U(t-t')\Ncal_\l(\TT;z_1,\dots,z_{j+1})(t')\,dt'\Bigr\|_{X^{s,b}_T}
\le T^{0+} C^j \delta^{(2s+2)(j-3)-1}\prod_{k=1}^{j+1}\|z_k\|_{X^{s,b}_T},
\]
\DIFadd{which holds for every $j\ge 1$, every $\TT\in\Tr_j$, and every $\l\in\{1,\dots,5\}$ (Proposition~\ref{prop:bourgL2}). The decisive feature of this bound is the exponent $(2s+2)(j-3)-1$: it is summable over $j$ for any }\DIFaddend $s>-1$\DIFdelbegin \DIFdel{and transfers the result back to the original Novikov-Veselov
equation . In this way, one obtains a sharp subcritical local well-posedness theory for \eqref{eq:NV} by purely perturbative means , with no appeal to inverse scattering or to the
complete integrability machinery.
}\DIFdelend \DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{, provided the gauge parameter $\delta$ is taken small in terms of the size of the data. This is the precise point at which the scaling exponent $s_c=-1$ becomes operational in the analysis. Proposition~\ref{prop:bourgL2} is in turn reduced, through the standard frequency-restricted-estimate machinery, to bounds on integrals over level sets $\{\Phi-\alpha=0\}$, uniformly in $\alpha$. Two regimes appear in the resonance geometry of $\Phi$: a generic non-degenerate stratum, and a stationary locus on which two input frequencies are nearly aligned. Lemma~\ref{lem:prop_resonance} treats both, by a direct change of variables on the first and a parameter-dependent application of Morse's lemma on the second. The argument then unfolds across the five algebraic tree types and seven case-by-case configurations.
}

\medskip

\textbf{\DIFadd{Returning to \eqref{eq:NV}: inversion of the gauge and density.}}
\DIFadd{To carry the well-posedness statement back to \eqref{eq:NV} we rely on two ingredients. Lemma~\ref{LEM:D} establishes that $\Gc_\delta$ and its inverse are bi-Lipschitz, in fact analytic, on suitable balls of $H^s$. Proposition~\ref{PRO:equiv} then shows that any smooth solution of \eqref{eq:NV} is mapped by $\Gc_\delta$ to the unique solution of \eqref{z1} with matching gauged data. Smoothing the initial datum and combining the high-regularity well-posedness theory of \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{angelopolous,kazeykinamunoz1,kazeykinamunoz2} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
with uniform-in-$n$ control on the gauged flow upgrades, by density, the smooth-level equivalence to a continuous-in-$H^s$ statement, which is exactly Theorem~\ref{thm:main_intro}.
}

\medskip

\textbf{\DIFadd{Appendix: analyticity in the range $s>-\tfrac34$.}}
\DIFadd{A short appendix is devoted to the (already-known) range $s>-\tfrac34$. There the gauge is not needed: the same frequency-restricted estimates already yield a contraction in $X^{s,b}$ directly for \eqref{eq:NV} when $E\neq 0$. This both recovers the result of Adams and Gr\"unrock \mbox{%DIFAUXCMD
\cite{adamsgrunrock} }\hskip0pt%DIFAUXCMD
(originally stated for $E=0$) and promotes the data-to-solution map of Theorem~\ref{thm:main_intro} from continuous to analytic in that range.
}

\medskip


\subsection{\DIFadd{Organization and notation}}

\DIFadd{The remainder of the paper is structured as follows. Section~\ref{sec:prem} sets up the tree formalism underlying the infinite normal-form expansion, derives the normal-form equation for \eqref{eq:NV}, and constructs the gauge transform $\Gc_\delta$, culminating in the gauged equation \eqref{z1}. Section~\ref{sec:multi} introduces the Fourier restriction space $X^{s,b}$, presents the frequency-restricted estimate lemmas, and proves the master multilinear bound Proposition~\ref{prop:bourgL2}; the necessary input on the resonance geometry is gathered in Lemma~\ref{lem:prop_resonance}. Section~\ref{sec:lwp} develops the local theory for the gauged equation (Proposition~\ref{prop:lwp_rnv}), records the analyticity of the gauge map (Lemma~\ref{LEM:D}), proves the equivalence between \eqref{eq:NV} and \eqref{z1} for smooth solutions (Proposition~\ref{PRO:equiv}), and closes the density argument that yields Theorem~\ref{thm:main_intro}. Appendix~\ref{sec:s-34} handles the auxiliary regime $s>-\tfrac34$ and refines the regularity of the data-to-solution map there.
}\DIFaddend 

\bigskip

\DIFaddbegin \DIFadd{We conclude with the notation used throughout the paper. For $A,B\in\R$, the symbol $A\lesssim B$ means $A\le C B$ for some $C>0$, while $A\sim B$ stands for the two-sided bound $A\lesssim B\lesssim A$; the slightly stronger comparability $\max(|A|,|B|)\gg\big||A|-|B|\big|$ is denoted $A\simeq B$. We write $\alpha+$ and $\alpha-$ for $\alpha+\varepsilon$ and $\alpha-\varepsilon$, respectively, with $\varepsilon>0$ chosen sufficiently small. The plane $\R^2$ is identified with $\C$ via $\xi=(\xi_1,\xi_2)\simeq\xi_1+i\xi_2$, and $\partial=\tfrac12(\partial_x+i\partial_y)$, $\overline\partial=\tfrac12(\partial_x-i\partial_y)$ are the corresponding Wirtinger derivatives. We use the Japanese bracket $\jap{\xi}:=(1+|\xi|^2)^{1/2}$, and we denote by $\mathcal{F}$ and $\mathcal{F}^{-1}$ (or $\widehat{\cdot}$) the spatial Fourier transform and its inverse on $\R^2$. The phase symbol attached to the quadratic part of \eqref{eq:NV} is $\Phi=\Phi_0+E\,\Phi_E$, with $\Phi_0$ and $\Phi_E$ as in Section~1.2, and $K(\xi_1,\xi_2)=\xi_1\cdot\xi_2/(|\xi_1|^2|\xi_2|^2)$ is the bilinear multiplier appearing in \eqref{eq:NV2}. For $j\ge 1$, we let $\Tr_j$ stand for the set of decorated binary trees of order $j$ defined in Section~\ref{sec:prem}, and we write $\Ncal_\l(\TT;\,\cdot\,)$ for the multilinear operator associated with a tree $\TT$ and a type label $\l\in\{1,\dots,5\}$. The usual Sobolev and Schwartz spaces are $H^s(\R^2)$, $\mathcal{S}(\R^2)$, $\mathcal{S}'(\R^2)$; finally, $X^{s,b}=X^{s,b}(\R\times\R^2)$ denotes the Bourgain-type Fourier restriction space adapted to the dispersion symbol of \eqref{eq:NV}, introduced in Section~\ref{sec:multi}.
}
